The Second Blast and the Soul's Persistence
Describes the second blast and argues for the soul's persistence using Subki's opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the second blast of the trumpet and the rational proof (istishab) for the soul's continuous existence after death.

Core Idea
The soul persists after death based on the principle of continuity (istishab) and the events of the grave.

This lesson explores two related topics from the sam‘iyyat (audibles) of the afterlife: the second trumpet blast that reunites souls with bodies, and a key theological argument for the soul's persistence (baqa’) after death. Drawing on Imam al-Subki’s reasoning, we see how a legal principle (istishab) is applied to establish a doctrine about the soul's state between death and resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second blast
Read the description of the second blast: Allah gathers souls in the trumpet, comes out to bodies without error. Note the time gap of forty years between blasts.
This is a concrete event in the afterlife.
2
Learn al-Subki's argument
Al-Subki argues for the soul's persistence using istishab: the principle that something remains as it was until evidence changes it. The evidence includes the soul's experience in the grave (questioning, bliss/punishment).
The verse 'except whom Allah wills' is an exception.
3
Recognize the scholarly endorsement
The author states that al-Subki's view is the chosen one among the people of truth, and al-Subki is mentioned due to his mastery of both rational and transmitted sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات الصور النفخة الثانية، وتُسمَّى نفخة البعث، فيجمع الله الأرواح في الصور عند النفخة الثانية، وفيه نَقْبٌ بعددها، فتخرج منه الأرواح إلى أجسادها، فلا تُخطئ روح جسدها، وبين النفختين أربعون عاماً على ما في بعض الطرق. قوله : (وَاسْتَظْهَرَ السُّبْكِي بَقَاهَا اللَّذْ عُرِف بتخفيف الياء وتسهيل ألف بقاها، وتسكين الذال لغة في (الذي) اختار الإمام تقي الدين السبكي في تفسيره المسمَّى بـ «الدر النظيم» من هذا الاختلافِ القول ببقائها الذي عهد سابقاً؛ لأنهم اتفقوا على بقائها بعد الموت لسؤالها في القبر وجوابها وتنعيمها أو تعذيبها فيه، والأصل في كل باق استمراره حتى يظهر ما يصرف عنه، فالدليل على بقائها الاستصحاب، فتكون من المستثنى بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا مَن شَاءَ اللهُ﴾ [النَّمل: ۸۷]، وما قاله السبكي هو المختار عند أهل الحق، وإنما خصه المصنف بالذكر لتبحُرِه في الفنون حتى أحاط بالمعقول والمنقول.
The sam‘iyyat: the second blast of the trumpet, called the blast of resurrection; Allah gathers the souls in the trumpet at the second blast, and in it there are holes according to their number, so the souls come out from it to their bodies, and no soul misses its body. Between the two blasts is forty years according to some narrations. His statement: (And al-Subki favored its persistence, which is known with the lightening of the ya’ and the simplification of the alif of baqaha, and the sukun of the dhal is a dialect in (alladhi)). Imam Taqi al-Din al-Subki chose in his commentary called al-Durr al-Nazim from this disagreement the opinion of its persistence as previously known, because they agreed upon its persistence after death due to its questioning in the grave, its answering, and its being given bliss or punishment therein. The principle regarding every abiding thing is its continuity until something appears that turns it away, so the evidence for its persistence is istishab. Thus it is among that which is excepted by His statement, exalted: 'Except whom Allah wills' [al-Naml:87]. What al-Subki said is the chosen view according to the people of truth, and the author specifically mentioned him due to his deep knowledge in the various fields until he encompassed the rational and transmitted sciences.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage first describes the second blast of the trumpet (the blast of resurrection), where Allah gathers the souls in the trumpet and then sends them to their bodies without error. It mentions that between the two blasts there are forty years according to some narrations. Then it comments on the line from the poem: 'And al-Subki favored its persistence', explaining that Imam Taqi al-Din al-Subki, in his tafsir al-Durr al-Nazim, chose the view that the soul persists after death. The reason given is that all scholars agree that the soul remains after death because it is questioned in the grave, responds, and experiences bliss or punishment. The author applies a legal principle (istishab): anything that continues to exist remains as it was until there is evidence to the contrary. Thus, the soul's persistence is based on this principle, and it is considered part of the exception in the Quranic verse 'except whom Allah wills'. The author concludes that al-Subki's view is the one chosen by the people of truth, and Ibn al-Muqri mentions al-Subki specifically because of his mastery in both rational and transmitted sciences.

Key Terms
نفخة البعث
استصحاب
سبكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the soul ceases to exist after death until resurrection.
Correction: The passage argues that the soul persists based on istishab (continuity) and agreement on its experience in the grave (questioning, bliss/punishment).

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence al-Subki uses for the soul's persistence after death?
A) Direct Qur'anic stating the soul is immortal
B) Istishab (continuity of the previous state) and agreement on events in the grave
C) A rational proof that the soul is non-physical
D) Consensus that the soul returns to the body before resurrection

Key Takeaway
The soul persists after death based on the principle of istishab and the universally accepted events of the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage affirms that your soul will persist after death and immediately face bliss or punishment. If you truly believed this will happen tonight, what would you do differently in the next hour?
?
Al-Subki used the principle of istishab—assuming continuity unless proven otherwise. Is there a harmful habit or belief you have assumed cannot change? What evidence do you have that it must continue, and what would it take to break that continuity?
?
The exception 'except whom Allah wills' reminds us that even the soul's persistence is under Allah's control. Have you ever relied on your own efforts or knowledge as if they were guaranteed? How can you live today in complete dependence on Allah's will rather than your own plans?

The Tailbone: Between Annihilation and Eternal Preservation
Defines the tailbone and presents the two views on its annihilation, with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether the tailbone perishes or remains eternally, and the evidence cited for each.

Core Idea
The tailbone is a unique bone that the earth never consumes, serving as the basis for resurrection.

This passage introduces a fascinating point of theological and eschatological discussion: the fate of the tailbone (ʿajb al-dhanab). Is it subject to decay like the rest of the body, or does it persist miraculously as the starting point for resurrection? This lesson will clarify the definitions, the positions of key scholars, and the scriptural proofs they employed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic source and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the detailed linguistic analysis at the start.
2
Focus on the definition of the tailbone
Identify what the tailbone is anatomically and linguistically. Note it is compared to the soul and why.
The comparison to the soul sets up the two views.
3
Understand the two views
Identify al-Muzani's view (annihilation) and the stronger view (persistence). Note the evidence each uses.
Al-Muzani uses the Quran; the hadith support persistence.
4
Analyze the evidence for each view
Compare the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing' with the hadith that the tailbone does not decay. Why does the author favor the hadith?
Consider the principle that specific evidence can qualify general evidence.
5
Consider the debate on the cause of its persistence
Examine the discussion on whether persistence is devotional or based on a cause. Understand why the devotional view is preferred.
The proposed cause (a sign for angels) is deemed weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلافُ في فناءِ عَجْبِ الذَّنَبِ عَجْبُ الذَّنَبْ كَالرُّوْحِ لَكِنْ صَحَّحَا المُزَنِيُّ لِلْبِلَى وَوَضَّحَا قوله : (عَجْبُ الذَّنَبْ كَالرُّوْحِ) (العجب) بفتح العين وسكون الجيم وآخره باء موحدة وقد تُبدل ميماً، وبعضهم يحكي تثليث أوله فيهما، فلغاته ست. وإضافته للذَّنَب من إضافة المماثل لمماثله فقولهم : عَجب الذنب : معناه عَجْبُ شبيه بالذنب، وهو عظم كالخردلة في آخر سلسلة الظهر في العُصْعُص مختص بالإنسان كمغرز الذنب للدابة، وهو بكسر الراء من باب (ضرب)، وتشبيهه بالروح في جريان الاختلاف في الفناء على قولين والمشهور منهما أنه لا يفنى لكن لا بقيد وقت النفخ، وإن كان الخلاف في المشبه به مُقيداً به، كما صرح به المصنف في قوله : (وفي فنا النفس لدى النفخ اختلف). قوله: (لَكِنْ صَحْحًا المُزَنِيُّ لِلْبِلَى) أي: لكن صحح الإمام إسماعيل بن يحيى المزني، وهو منسوبٌ لـ (مُزَينة) اسم قبيلة القول بأنَّ عَجب الذنب يبلى ويفني، تمسكاً بظاهر قوله تعالى : كُلُّ مَنْ عَلَيْهَا فَانٍ ﴾ [الرَّحمن (٢٦] ، وفناء الكل يستلزم فناء الجزء. وقوله : (وَوَضَحَا) أي : بين صحة ما ذهب إليه ووافقه ابن قتيبة، وقال: إنه آخر ما يبلى من الميت والأقوى في النظر أنه لا يبلى لحديث الصحيحين: «ليس من الإنسان شيء إلا يبلى إلا عظماً واحداً؛ وهو عجب الذنب، منه خُلِقَ الخلقُ يوم القيامة»، ولحديث مسلم : «كلُّ ابن آدم يأكله التراب إلا عجب الذنب منه خُلِق، ومنه يُرَكَّبُ»، وفي حديثه الآخر: «إن في الإنسان عظماً لا تأكله الأرض أبداً»، واختلف هل بقاؤه تعبدي أو مُعلَّل، والأرجح أنه تعبدي لضعف ما علل به القائل بأنه معلل، فإنه علله بجواز كونه جعل علامة للملائكة الموكلين بالإعادة على إحياء كل إنسان بجواهره التي كانت في الدنيا، ووجه ضعفه أن الملائكة لا يخفى عليهم هذا الأمر، مع أنهم يعيدون كلَّ إنسان بجواهره بأمر الله على أنه يجوز اللَّبْسُ فيه نفسه
The disagreement over the annihilation of the tailbone. The tailbone is like the soul, but al-Muzani corrected (the view) of decay and clarified it. His statement: (The tailbone is like the soul). (al-‘Ajb) with a fatha on the ‘ayn and sukun on the jim, ending with a ba’ with one dot, and it may be replaced with a mim; some relate the triplication of its first letter in both, so it has six dialects. Its addition to al-dhanab is an addition of a similar to its similar, so they say: ‘ajb al-dhanab – its meaning is a ‘ajb similar to the tail, and it is a bone like a mustard seed at the end of the spinal column in the coccyx, specific to humans, like the insertion of the tail of an animal; it (the word) is with a kasra on the ra’ from the pattern of daraba. Its being likened to the soul is due to the flow of disagreement over annihilation on two views, the more famous of which is that it does not perish, but not restricted to the time of the blast, even if the disagreement regarding that which is likened (the soul) is restricted to it, as the author clarified in his statement: 'And regarding the annihilation of the soul at the blast, there is disagreement.' His statement: (but al-Muzani corrected for decay) means: but Imam Isma‘il ibn Yahya al-Muzani – attributed to (Muzayna) the name of a tribe – corrected the view that the tailbone decays and perishes, adhering to the apparent meaning of His statement, exalted: 'Everyone upon it is perishing' [al-Rahman:26], and the annihilation of the whole necessitates the annihilation of the part. And his statement: (and clarified) means: he made clear the correctness of what he went to, and Ibn Qutayba agreed with him, and he said: It is the last thing that decays from the deceased. But the stronger view in consideration is that it does not decay due to the hadith of the two Sahihs: 'There is nothing of a person that does not decay except one bone, and it is the tailbone; from it the creation is created on the Day of Resurrection.' And due to the hadith of Muslim: 'Every son of Adam is consumed by the earth except the tailbone; from it he was created, and from it he will be reconstituted.' And in his other hadith: 'Verily, in a person there is a bone that the earth never consumes.' And they disagreed whether its persistence is devotional (ta‘abbudi) or based on a cause (mu‘allal); the more likely is that it is devotional due to the weakness of what was used as cause by those who claim it is based on a cause. For they gave as cause that it might be made a sign for the angels charged with resurrection to revive each person with his substances that were in the world. The weakness of this is that the angels are not unaware of this matter, especially since they resurrect each person with his substances by the command of Allah, and it is possible that there is confusion in this matter itself.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage begins a new subject: the disagreement over the annihilation of the tailbone (ʿajb al-dhanab). It first quotes a line from the poem: 'The tailbone is like the soul, but al-Muzani corrected the view of decay and clarified it.' Then it provides a detailed definition and linguistic analysis of the word 'ajb, noting its various pronunciations. It explains that the tailbone is a small bone at the end of the spine, like a mustard seed, specific to humans, analogous to the insertion of an animal's tail. The likening of the tailbone to the soul refers to the fact that there are two views regarding its annihilation, just as there are two views regarding the soul's annihilation at the time of the blast. The more famous view is that the tailbone does not perish, but this view is not restricted to the time of the blast, unlike the disagreement about the soul. It then explains al-Muzani's position: he held that the tailbone decays and perishes, based on the apparent meaning of the verse 'Everyone upon it is perishing' [al-Rahman:26], arguing that the annihilation of the whole (all creatures) implies the annihilation of the part (the tailbone). Al-Muzani was supported by Ibn Qutayba, who said it is the last part to decay. However, the author deems the stronger view to be that it does not decay, citing three hadiths from Bukhari and Muslim: (1) Nothing decays except one bone, the tailbone, from which creation is recreated; (2) Every part is consumed except the tailbone, from which it was created and reconstituted; (3) There is a bone that the earth never consumes. Then the author discusses whether its persistence is devotional (taʿabbudi, based purely on divine command) or based on a cause (muʿallal, with a rational explanation). He concludes that devotional is more likely because the proposed cause – that it serves as a sign for the angels to identify each person's substances – is weak: angels are not ignorant of this matter, and they revive each person by God's command, and confusion is still possible.

Key Terms
عجب الذنب
مزني
إعادة
تعبدي
معلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tailbone perishes along with the rest of the body.
Correction: Authentic hadith clearly state that the tailbone is the one bone that does not decay and is the basis for resurrection.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence for the view that the tailbone does not perish?
A) The Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing'
B) Hadiths from Bukhari and Muslim stating it does not decay
C) Consensus of the scholars
D) Rational argument that it must remain for resurrection

Key Takeaway
The tailbone is a miraculous bone that does not decay, serving as the origin for bodily resurrection, with strong evidence from authentic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your very bones are preserved for resurrection, how would that change the way you use your limbs and body today? What small, hidden sin have you been committing with a part of your body that will bear witness against you?
?
The tailbone is a tiny, unseen bone that Allah preserves as the seed of your return. What unseen acts of obedience or disobedience have you stored up that will be resurrected with you? Are you ready for that moment when every atom of your existence is reassembled to face your Lord?
?
You claim to believe in the resurrection, but do your daily choices reflect that conviction? If you knew that your tailbone was the proof that your body will be rebuilt, would you treat your body as a trust to be returned to Allah intact, or as a vessel for your own desires?

Specifying the General Verse 'Everything Perishes'
Explanation of specifying the general verse to exclude the soul and tailbone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent generality of the Quranic verse 'Everything perishes' is specified to allow for the persistence of the soul and tailbone.

Core Idea
General Quranic verses can be specified by other texts; they are not absolute.

This lesson addresses a common question: if everything perishes except Allah, how can the soul and tailbone persist? The author of the Jawharah points out that scholars have specified the general meaning of such verses through other scriptural evidence. Let's see how this is done.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author begins by quoting a line from the poem.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection and resolution
The objection comes from the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'. The resolution is that scholars have specified (takhsis) the generality of that verse.
The phrase 'قد خصصوا عمومه' is key.
3
Understand the principle of specification
In Islamic theology, general statements in revelation can be narrowed by other revealed texts. This allows for exceptions like the soul and tailbone.
This is a standard principle of usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ هَالِكٌ قَدْ خَصَّصُوا عُمُوْمَهُ فَاطْلُبْ لِمَا قَدْ لَخَصُوا قوله : (وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ هَالِكُ قَدْ خَصَّصُوا عُمُوْمَهُ) لما كان القول ببقاء الروح وعجب الذنب هو الراجح، أشار المصنف إلى الجواب عما يرد عليه كقوله تعالى : كُلُّ شَىءٍ هَا لِك إِلَّا وَجْهَهُ ﴾ [القصص : ۸۸] ؛ إذ مقتضاه أن كل ما سواه تعالى محكوم عليه بالهلاك.
And everything is perishing; they have specified its generality, so seek what they have summarized. His statement: (And everything is perishing; they have specified its generality). Since the view of the persistence of the soul and the tailbone is the stronger one, the author indicated the answer to what might be objected against it, like His statement, exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' [al-Qasas:88]; for its apparent meaning is that everything other than Him, exalted, is subject to perishing.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if the soul and tailbone persist, how do we understand the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' [al-Qasas:88], which seems to say that all creatures perish? The author quotes a line from the poem: 'And everything is perishing; they have specified its generality, so seek what they have summarized.' This means that scholars have specified the general meaning of such verses by other texts that indicate some things do not perish. Since the stronger view is that the soul and tailbone persist, these items are exceptions to the general rule of perishing. The author indicates that this is the answer to the objection: the generality of the verse is not absolute; it is specified by the evidence for the persistence of the soul and tailbone.

Key Terms
عموم
تخصيص
هلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Everything perishes' must be interpreted literally and absolutely, so nothing can persist.
Correction: General Qur'anic verses can be specified by other revealed texts; the persistence of the soul and tailbone is supported by evidence.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the conflict between the persistence of the soul/tailbone and the verse 'Everything perishes'?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) The verse's generality is specified by other texts
C) The soul and tailbone are not part of 'everything'
D) The verse applies only to the afterlife

Key Takeaway
The generality of a Quranic verse can be specified by other scriptural evidence, allowing for exceptions such as the persistence of the soul and tailbone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face,' do you truly feel that everything you love will perish? How does the doctrine of the persistence of the soul change your perspective on your attachments in this life?
?
Are you living as if only Allah's Face will remain? What would change in your daily choices if you fully internalized that your soul will persist after your body perishes?
?
The scholars specified the general verse based on other evidence. Do you trust that the Quran and Sunnah together give a complete picture, even when a single verse seems to contradict a belief? How does this affect your approach to understanding difficult texts?

The Survival of the Coccyx Bone
Definition of 'ajb al-dhanab and discussion of its survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate about the survival of the coccyx bone (عجب الذنب), the evidence from hadith, and the distinction between devotional and rational explanations.

Core Idea
The coccyx bone's survival is a revealed truth, not a rational deduction.

This lesson examines a specific eschatological detail: the fate of a small bone at the base of the spine called 'ajb al-dhanab. While some scholars argued it decays based on a general Quranic verse, authentic hadith assert it remains intact as the seed of resurrection. The author also explains why this survival is best understood as a devotional truth beyond human reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition and initial disagreement
Read the definition of 'ajb al-dhanab and note the two opinions on its annihilation: the famous view that it does not perish, and al-Muzani's view that it does.
The bone is compared to the soul in terms of disagreement.
2
Examine the hadith evidence for survival
Study the hadith from al-Bukhari and Muslim that explicitly state the coccyx does not decay and is the basis for resurrection. This evidence outweighs the general Quranic verse.
The hadith are considered stronger by the author.
3
Analyze the debate: devotional vs. rational
Understand why the author favors the devotional (ta'abbudi) view over the rational (mu'allal) view, noting the weakness of the rational argument about angels.
This shows the priority of revelation over human reasoning in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبيه بالذنب، وهو عظم كالخردلة في آخر سلسلة الظهر في العُصْعُص مختص بالإنسان كمغرز الذنب للدابة، وهو بكسر الراء من باب (ضرب)، وتشبيهه بالروح في جريان الاختلاف في الفناء على قولين والمشهور منهما أنه لا يفنى لكن لا بقيد وقت النفخ، وإن كان الخلاف في المشبه به مُقيداً به، كما صرح به المصنف في قوله : (وفي فنا النفس لدى النفخ اختلف).
قوله: (لَكِنْ صَحْحًا المُزَنِيُّ لِلْبِلَى) أي: لكن صحح الإمام إسماعيل بن يحيى المزني، وهو منسوبٌ لـ (مُزَينة) اسم قبيلة القول بأنَّ عَجب الذنب يبلى ويفني، تمسكاً بظاهر قوله تعالى : كُلُّ مَنْ عَلَيْهَا فَانٍ ﴾ [الرَّحمن (٢٦] ، وفناء الكل يستلزم فناء الجزء.
وقوله : (وَوَضَحَا) أي : بين صحة ما ذهب إليه ووافقه ابن قتيبة، وقال: إنه آخر ما يبلى من الميت والأقوى في النظر أنه لا يبلى لحديث الصحيحين»: ليس من الإنسان شيء إلا يبلى إلا عظماً واحداً؛ وهو عجب الذنب، منه خُلِقَ الخلقُ يوم القيامة» (۱) ، ولحديث مسلم : كلُّ ابن آدم يأكله التراب إلا عجب الذنب منه خُلِق، ومنه يُرَكَّبُ (۲) ، وفي حديثه الآخر: إن في الإنسان عظماً لا تأكله الأرض أبداً (۳) ، واختلف هل بقاؤه تعبدي أو مُعلَّل، والأرجح أنه تعبدي لضعف ما علل به القائل بأنه معلل، فإنه علله بجواز كونه جعل علامة للملائكة الموكلين بالإعادة على إحياء كل إنسان بجواهره التي كانت في الدنيا، ووجه ضعفه أن الملائكة لا يخفى عليهم هذا الأمر، مع أنهم يعيدون كلَّ إنسان بجواهره بأمر الله على أنه يجوز اللَّبْسُ فيه نفسه
Similar to the tail, and it is a bone like a mustard seed at the end of the spinal column in the coccyx, specific to humans, like the insertion of the tail for an animal. It is with a kasrah on the ra' from the pattern (daraba). Its comparison to the soul [applies] regarding the occurrence of disagreement about annihilation (fana') on two opinions, the more well-known of which is that it does not perish, but not restricted to the time of the Blast, even though the disagreement concerning the thing compared is restricted to it, as the author stated in his words: 'And regarding the annihilation of the soul at the time of the Blast, they differed.' His words: 'But al-Muzani declared correct [the view of] decay' meaning: But Imam Isma'il ibn Yahya al-Muzani, who is attributed to (Muzayna) the name of a tribe, declared correct the opinion that the coccyx decays and perishes, adhering to the apparent meaning of His statement, Exalted: 'Everyone upon it is perishing' [ar-Rahman 26], and the annihilation of the whole necessitates the annihilation of the part. And his words: 'And he clarified' meaning: he made clear the correctness of what he went to, and Ibn Qutaybah agreed with him, and said: It is the last thing that decays from the dead. But the stronger [view] in consideration is that it does not decay, due to the hadith of the Two Sahihs: 'There is nothing of the human that does not decay except one bone, which is the coccyx; from it creation was created on the Day of Resurrection,' and due to the hadith of Muslim: 'Every son of Adam is eaten by the earth except the coccyx; from it he was created, and from it he will be recomposed,' and in his other hadith: 'There is in the human a bone that the earth will never eat.' And they differed whether its survival is devotional (ta'abbudi) or rational (mu'allal). The preferable view is that it is devotional due to the weakness of what the one who said it is rational argued with, for he argued by the possibility that it was made a sign for the angels entrusted with resurrection to revive every human with his atoms that were in the world. The weakness of this is that the angels are not unaware of this matter, especially since they revive every human with his atoms by the command of God, and it is possible for confusion to occur regarding it itself.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage defines 'ajb al-dhanab (the coccyx bone) and discusses the theological debate over its annihilation (fana') and survival (baqa'). The author begins by defining 'ajb al-dhanab as a small bone at the base of the spine, unique to humans, and compares its status to the soul: both are subjects of disagreement regarding whether they perish. The more famous view is that the coccyx does not perish, but this survival is not tied to a specific time like the Blast of the Trumpet. Next, the author explains al-Muzani's view that the coccyx does decay, based on the Quranic verse 'Everyone upon it is perishing' (55:26), arguing that the whole includes the part. However, the author clarifies that the stronger position, supported by authentic hadith from al-Bukhari and Muslim, is that the coccyx does not decay. The hadith explicitly state that this bone will not be eaten by the earth and will be the basis for resurrection. The author then notes a further disagreement: whether the survival of the coccyx is a devotional matter (ta'abbudi, accepted without rational explanation) or rationally explicable (mu'allal). The preferred view is that it is devotional, because the rational argument (that it serves as a marker for angels) is weak: angels do not need such a marker, as they already know everything by God's command.

Key Terms
عجب الذنب
الفناء
التعبدي
المعلل
الصحيحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The coccyx's survival is a purely scientific claim that can be proven or disproven empirically.
Correction: The survival of the coccyx is a theological matter based on hadith (revealed texts), with rational arguments considered weak. It is accepted as a devotional truth, not a scientific fact.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the stronger opinion regarding the annihilation (fana') of 'ajb al-dhanab?
A) It perishes, as al-Muzani held.
B) It does not perish, supported by hadith.
C) It perishes only at the Blast of the Trumpet.
D) Its survival is purely rational (mu'allal).

Key Takeaway
The coccyx bone (عجب الذنب) is understood in Sunni theology as a divinely preserved seed for resurrection, a truth accepted on revelation rather than reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the survival of this bone is 'devotional' (تعبدي), do you feel a tension in your heart? Are you tempted to seek a scientific rationale for everything in your faith, or can you submit to revelation without proof?
?
Do you truly believe that a single bone will be the starting point of your resurrection, or do you secretly rationalize it as a metaphor? What would change in your daily life if you internalized that your physical resurrection is tied to a specific, divinely preserved part of you?
?
If an angel asked you right now, 'What is stronger in your heart: the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) or the conclusions of modern anatomy?' — what would your honest answer be, and why?

Prohibition of Investigating the Nature of the Soul
The author forbids delving into the soul's essence and discusses its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on investigating the soul's essence and the two scholarly positions of dislike (karaha) and prohibition (hurma).

Core Idea
The soul's essence is Divine secret; inquiry is either disliked or forbidden.

This lesson addresses a key methodological principle in Islamic theology: the prohibition against probing into the nature of the soul. The author presents the text from Jawhara as a warning, and the commentators discuss whether the prohibition indicates dislike or outright impermissibility. We will examine the evidence and understand what is permitted regarding the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's warning and the two interpretations.
Pay attention to the variant reading with nun vs ta.
2
Identify the two rulings
Notice that the commentator says it is makruh due to lack of tawqif, but al-Junayd says it is haram.
3
Consider the Quranic evidence
The verse from Surat al-Isra' is cited to show the soul is from God's command, beyond human grasp.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this principle protects Islamic theology from speculation.
Affirm existence, refrain from essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا نَخُضْ فِي الرُّوحِ إِذْ مَا وَرَدَا نَضٌ عَنِ الشارع لَكِنْ وُجِدًا
قوله : (وَلَا نَخُضْ في الرُّوْحِ) أي : ولا نخض نحن معاشر جمهور المحققين في بيان حقيقة الروح ، هكذا في شرح المصنف (۳) ، ومقتضى هذا أن المتن يقرأ بالنون والشائع قراءته بالتاء التي للمخاطب، وحمل الشارح النهي على الكراهة حيث قال : فالخوض في بيان حقيقتها مكروه؛ لعدم التوقيف في ذلك. لكن كلام الجنيد يدلُّ على الحرمة، حيث قال : الروح شيء استأثر الله بعلمه، فلم يطلع عليه أحد من خلقه، فلا يجوز لعباده البحث عنها بأكثر من أنها موجودة، قال تعالى: ﴿وَيَسْتَلُونَكَ عَنِ الرُّوحِ قُلِ الرُّوحُ مِنْ أَمْرِ رَبِّي ﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٥، وفي ذلك إظهار لعجز
And do not delve into the soul, since no text has come from the Lawgiver, but it has been found. His words: 'And do not delve into the soul' meaning: we, the community of the majority of verifiers, do not delve into explaining the reality of the soul. This is how it is in the author's commentary. And the implication of this is that the text is read with a nun (first person plural) while the common reading is with a ta (second person singular). The commentator interpreted the prohibition as indicating dislike (karaha), where he said: Delving into explaining its reality is disliked due to the lack of authoritative transmission (tawqif) regarding it. But the words of al-Junayd indicate prohibition (hurma), where he said: The soul is a thing that God has exclusively kept His knowledge, so He has not disclosed it to any of His creation. Therefore, it is not permissible for His servants to research it beyond the fact that it exists. He said, Exalted: 'And they ask you about the soul. Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord' [al-Isra' 85], and in that is a display of incapacity.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage warns against delving into the essence (haqiqa) of the soul (ruh). The author presents the text as a prohibition. He notes that the variant reading uses the first-person plural (naf'il) indicating the community of scholars refrains, while the common reading is second-person singular (taf'il) addressing the student. The commentator (al-Sanusi or the author of Tuhfat al-Mureed, likely al-Bajuri or al-Dasuqi) interprets the prohibition as a matter of dislike (karaha) because there is no transmitted text (tawqif) specifying its nature. However, al-Junayd's statement indicates outright prohibition (hurma), as he asserts that God has kept the knowledge of the soul exclusively to Himself, and thus it is not permissible for His servants to investigate it beyond affirming its existence. The Quranic verse 'Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord' (17:85) is cited as evidence that the soul's nature is beyond human grasp, showing human incapacity. The author thus presents two positions: one of dislike and one of prohibition, with the latter being stronger based on al-Junayd's authority.

Key Terms
الروح
الخوض
الحقيقة
التوقيف
الكراهة
الحرمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is completely forbidden to speak about the soul at all, even its existence.
Correction: The prohibition applies to investigating its essence (haqiqa), but affirming its existence is permissible and necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on delving into the essence of the soul based on al-Junayd's statement?
A) It is recommended (mandub).
B) It is disliked (makruh).
C) It is forbidden (haram).
D) It is obligatory (wajib).

Key Takeaway
The soul's essence is a Divine secret; Muslims must affirm its existence but refrain from speculating about its reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cannot fully understand a matter of your faith, do you feel a sense of loss or frustration? How does this passage's command to refrain from investigating the soul's nature challenge your need for intellectual certainty? Will you accept God's exclusive knowledge as a form of worship?
?
The soul is intimately yours, yet its true essence is kept from you. How does this make you view your own existence? Are you willing to live with that mystery, or do you secretly desire to uncover it? What would change in your daily life if you truly surrendered this curiosity?
?
Al-Junayd said the soul is something God kept exclusively to Himself. Have you ever tried to ‘know’ yourself apart from God’s revelation? In what areas of your life do you still insist on understanding before submitting? After this lesson, what step will you take to trust God more with the hidden aspects of your own being?

The General Rule of Perishing and Its Exceptions
The author explains the exception to 'everything perishes' and defines general and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile the verse 'Everything is perishing' with the existence of eternal entities like Paradise and Hell, through either particularization or reinterpretation.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic rule of perishing is not absolute; it is either specified by hadith or denotes potential annihilation.

The apparent contradiction between the Qur'anic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' and the existence of eternal things like Paradise and Hell has been addressed by scholars in two main ways. This passage presents both responses, along with the defining of key terms such as 'general' and 'particularization'. Understanding these interpretations is essential for a correct theological perspective on Allah's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow. Note that the author first presents the majority view, then the later verifying scholars' view.
Pay attention to the poetic enumeration by al-Suyuti.
2
Identify the two scholarly responses
The first response considers the verse as a general statement that has been particularized by hadith. The second response interprets 'perishable' as 'liable to perish', avoiding the need for exception.
The second view is held by muhaqqiqun.
3
Understand the key terms
The passage defines 'general' (عام) and 'particularization' (تخصيص). Also note the term 'sam‘iyyāt' (matters known through transmission).
These terms are foundational in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why scholars preferred one view over the other. The first view maintains the literal meaning of the verse while accommodating hadith exceptions. The second view harmonizes without making exceptions.
Both views aim to preserve the integrity of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات وحاصل الجواب أن العلماء فَصَرُوا عموم ذلك على غير الأمور التي وردت الأحاديث باستثنائها ؛ كالروح وعجب الذنب وأجساد الأنبياء والشهداء والعرش والكرسي والجنة والنار والحور العين ونحو ذلك، وقد نظم الجلال السيوطي ثمانية بقوله : [من الطويل] ثَمَانِيَةٌ حُكْمُ البَقَاءِ يَعُمُّها مِنَ الخَلْقِ والباقُونَ فِي حَيِّنِ العَدَمُ هي العَرْسُ والكُرْسِيُّ نارُ وَجَنَّةٌ وعَجْبُ وأَرْواحُ كَذَا اللَّوْحُ والقَلَمُ وعلى هذا فتكون الآية من قبيل العام المخصوص والعام : لفظ يستغرق الصالح له بغير حصر والتخصيص قصرُ العام على بعض أفراده، وهذا الجواب لجماعة كابن عباس، وذهب محققو المتأخرين إلى أنه لا استثناء ولا تخصيص، وقالوا : معنى (هالك) قابل للهلاك، كما هو معنى (فان) أيضاً . وقوله : (فَاطْلُبْ لِمَا قَدْ لَخَصُوا أي فتوجه لما قد لخص العلماء؛ أي: من الأمور التي وردت الأحاديث باستثنائها، وقد تقدم بيانها
【Matters transmitted】 The summary of the answer is that the scholars restricted the generality of that to other than the matters which hadiths came exempting them, such as the soul, the coccyx, the bodies of prophets and martyrs, the Throne, the Footstool, Paradise, Hell, the Houris, and the like. Al-Jalal al-Suyuti versified eight [exceptions] in his poem: 'Eight [things] the ruling of permanence encompasses them among creation, and the ones remaining at the time of annihilation are the Throne, the Footstool, Fire and Paradise, the coccyx, and souls, likewise the Tablet and the Pen.' On this basis, the verse is of the type of a general that is particularized. 'General' (al-`amm) is an expression that encompasses all that which is suitable for it without limitation, and 'particularization' (al-takhsis) is restricting the general to some of its individuals. This answer is [from] a group like Ibn `Abbas. The verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) among the later ones held that there is no exemption and no particularization, and they said: the meaning of 'perishable' (halik) is liable to perish, just as it is the meaning of 'transient' (fan). And His saying: 'So seek that which they have summarized' (фа̄т̣либ ли ма̄ кад лах̣с̣асӯ) — i.e., direct yourself to that which the scholars have summarized, meaning from among the matters which hadiths came exempting, and their explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage addresses the sam`iyyat (matters known through transmission), specifically the apparent contradiction between the Qur’anic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88) and the existence of eternal things like Paradise and Hell. The author presents two scholarly responses. First, the dominant view: the verse is a general statement (عام) that has been particularized (مخصوص) by hadiths that exempt certain entities from perishing. The author lists examples: the soul, the coccyx (عجب الذنب), bodies of prophets and martyrs, the Throne, the Footstool, Paradise, Hell, the Houris. He then quotes al-Suyuti’s poetic enumeration of eight such entities: the Throne, Footstool, Hell, Paradise, the coccyx, souls, the Tablet, and the Pen. Following this, he defines 'general' (العام) as an expression encompassing all its eligible referents without counting, and 'particularization' (التخصيص) as restricting the general to some of its individuals. This view is attributed to a group including Ibn `Abbas. The second view, held by later verifying scholars (محققو المتأخرين), denies any exemption or particularization. They interpret 'perishable' (هالك) as 'liable to perish' — i.e., everything is inherently capable of annihilation, even if it is not actually annihilated. The passage then explains the line from the poem (فَاطْلُبْ لِمَا قَدْ لَخَصُوا) as an instruction to seek the matters that scholars have summarized, i.e., the exempted items already mentioned.

Key Terms
عام
مخصوص
تخصيص
السمعيات
هالك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse is absolute and denies any eternal creation, meaning everything except Allah will be completely annihilated.
Correction: The scholars clarify that the verse is a general statement with specified exceptions (first view) or that 'perishable' means 'liable to perish' (second view), so eternal creation like Paradise and Hell are not contradicted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'perishable' (هالك) according to the later verifying scholars?
A) Annihilated completely
B) Liable to perish
C) Eternally existing
D) Nonexistent in essence

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Everything is perishing' does not demand actual annihilation of all things; scholars interpret it either as a general rule with specific exceptions or as a statement of potential perishability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse says everything except Allah's Face perishes. Yet the scholars identify exceptions like the Throne and Paradise. Does knowing that some created things will never perish weaken your awe of Allah's absolute sovereignty, or does it deepen it? How does your answer reveal what you truly believe about Allah's power?
?
Both interpretations in this lesson—particularization and potentiality—aim to protect the verse from contradicting hadith. Which approach feels more honest to you when reading the Qur'an? Are you willing to accept a meaning that requires technical linguistic reasoning, or do you prefer a straightforward reading even if it raises questions? What does that preference say about your relationship with revelation?
?
If everything is 'liable to perish' but Allah preserves some things forever, what does that tell you about your own existence? You are not among the exceptions listed. How does this lesson change the way you think about your own mortality and what you are investing your life in? Will you live differently starting today, knowing that everything you cling to—except what Allah wills—is marked for annihilation?

Prohibition of Delving into the Soul's Reality
Prohibition and two opinions on discussing the soul's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly rulings on discussing the soul's essence and the evidence each uses.

Core Idea
The soul's true nature is known only to Allah; scholars differ on whether investigating it is disliked or forbidden.

This lesson examines the first part of the passage, where the author forbids delving into the soul's reality. You will encounter the two readings of the verb, the commentator's interpretation of the prohibition as merely disliked (makruh), and al-Junayd's view that it is strictly prohibited (haram) based on the Qur'an. By the end, you will see why refraining is the chosen position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: a prohibition, then two opinions, then supporting evidence and a conclusion.
The passage includes a poetic line and commentary.
2
Identify the two readings
Find where the author discusses the reading of the verb ('we do not delve' vs 'do not delve'). Understand that the commentary adopts the first person plural.
This grammatical detail affects the interpretation.
3
Compare the two rulings
Note the commentator's view (makruh) and al-Junayd's view (haram). What evidence does each use?
Remember the tawqif (revealed guidance) argument.
4
Analyze the Qur'anic evidence
Read the verse from Surah al-Isra' and the response about the Prophet's knowledge. Understand why delving beyond existence is problematic.
5
Reflect on the chosen position
The author concludes that refraining is the chosen view. Consider why this is the safer stance.
This sets the tone for the rest of the matn.

الرُّوحُ والخوضُ فيها ۹۳ - وَلَا نَخُضْ فِي الرُّوحِ إِذْ مَا وَرَدَا نَضٌ عَنِ الشارع لَكِنْ وُجِدًا قوله : (وَلَا نَخُضْ في الرُّوْحِ) أي : ولا نخض نحن معاشر جمهور المحققين في بيان حقيقة الروح ، هكذا في شرح المصنف ، ومقتضى هذا أن المتن يقرأ بالنون والشائع قراءته بالتاء التي للمخاطب، وحمل الشارح النهي على الكراهة حيث قال : فالخوض في بيان حقيقتها مكروه؛ لعدم التوقيف في ذلك. لكن كلام الجنيد يدلُّ على الحرمة، حيث قال : الروح شيء استأثر الله بعلمه، فلم يطلع عليه أحد من خلقه، فلا يجوز لعباده البحث عنها بأكثر من أنها موجودة، قال تعالى: ﴿وَيَسْتَلُونَكَ عَنِ الرُّوحِ قُلِ الرُّوحُ مِنْ أَمْرِ رَبِّي ﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٥]، وفي ذلك إظهار لعجز المرء حيث لم يعلم حقيقة نفسه التي بين جنبيه مع القطع بوجودها، ولم يخرج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدنيا حتى أطلعه الله تعالى على جميع ما أبهمه عنه من الروح وغيرها، مما يمكن علم البشر به لا على جميع معلوماته تعالى وإلا لزم مساواة الحادث للقديم، وما خالف ذلك نحو : ﴿وَلَا أَعْلَمُ الْغَيْبَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٠] محمول على أنه كان قبل أن يُكشف له عن ذلك، وما ذكر من عدم الخوض في الروح هو المختار؛ ولذلك صدر الناظم به فنمسك عن بيان حقيقتها، وبيان مقرّها من الجسد والمشهور عدم تعدّدِ الروح في كل جسد، وصَرَّح العِزُّ بن عبد السلام بأن في كل جسد روحين : إحداهما : روحُ اليقظة التي أجرى الله العادة بأنها إذا كانت في الجسد كان الإنسان مستيقظاً، فإذا خرجت منه ،نام ورأت تلك الروح المنامات. والأخرى : روحُ الحياة التي أجرى الله العادة بأنها إذا كانت في الجسد كان حيا، وإذا فارقته ،مات وهاتان الروحان في باطن الإنسان لا يعرف مقرَّهما إلا مَنْ أطلعه الله على ذلك . وقد كان بعض الأرواح يومَ اَلَسْتُ بِرَبِّكُمْ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۷۲] مقبلاً على بعض بالوجه، وبعضها مُولّياً ظهره لبعض، وبعضها جاعلاً جنبه لبعض، فالإقبال بالوجه غاية في المودة وعكسه بالظهر، وبالجنب بين ذلك، كما في «اليواقيت»، قال: ويُكشف لكثير عن ذلك كسهل بن عبد الله ، حتى إنهم يعرفون تلامذتهم إذ ذاك)، وفي الحديث: «الأرواح جُنودٌ مُجنَّدَةٌ، فما تعارف منها ائتلف، وما تناكر منها اختلف»
【The soul and delving into it】
93 – 'And do not delve into the soul since no text from the Lawgiver has come, but it has been found.'
His saying: 'And do not delve into the soul' – i.e., we, the community of the majority of verifying scholars, do not delve into explaining the reality of the soul. Thus it is in the commentary of the author. The implication is that the text is read with the nun (first person plural), but the common reading is with the ta (second person masculine). The commentator interpreted the prohibition as makruh (disliked) where he said: Delving into explaining its reality is disliked (makruh) due to the lack of [revealed] instruction (tawqif) concerning it. However, the speech of al-Junayd indicates prohibition (hurmah), where he said: The soul is a thing which Allah has kept exclusively for His knowledge, so He has not disclosed it to any of His creation. Therefore it is not permissible for His servants to investigate it beyond that it exists. Allah Most High says: 'And they ask you about the soul. Say: The soul is of the affair of my Lord' (17:85). In that is a display of man's incapability, for he does not know the reality of his own self which is between his sides while being certain of its existence. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not leave this world until Allah Most High had informed him of all that He had left ambiguous to him concerning the soul and other matters, of that which human knowledge is capable of – not of all of Allah's information, for that would entail the originated being equal to the Eternal. And what contradicts that, such as 'And I do not know the unseen' (6:50), is interpreted as before he was disclosed that. What is mentioned of not delving into the soul is the chosen position; and therefore the author began with it and we refrain from explaining its reality and explaining its locus in the body. The well-known [view] is that there are not multiple souls in each body. Al-`Izz ibn `Abd al-Salam explicitly stated that in each body there are two souls: one is the soul of wakefulness, which Allah's custom runs that if it is in the body, the person is awake; if it leaves it, he sleeps and sees dreams. The other is the soul of life, which Allah's custom runs that if it is in the body, he is alive; if it leaves it, he dies. These two souls are inside the person; no one knows their locus except one whom Allah has informed of that. And some souls on the day of 'Am I not your Lord?' (7:172) were facing some others with their faces, and some were turning their backs to others, and some were placing their sides to others – facing with the face is extreme in affection, and its opposite with the back, and with the side is between that – as in al-Yawaqit. He said: And many are made to know of that, such as Sahl ibn `Abdullah, to the extent that they recognize their students from that time. And in the hadith: 'Souls are like conscripted troops; those that recognize each other get along, and those that do not recognize each differ.'
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poetic line 'And do not delve into the soul since no text from the Lawgiver has come, but it has been found.' The author explains that the community of verifying scholars avoids discussing the true reality (haqiqa) of the soul. He notes two readings of the verb: with nun (first person plural, 'we do not delve') which matches the commentary's interpretation, though the common recitation is with ta (second person). The commentator (the author of the original commentary, likely al-Baijuri) considered the prohibition to be one of dislike (karaha) due to the absence of transmitted guidance (tawqif). However, he contrasts this with al-Junayd's statement that implies strict prohibition (hurmah), since the soul is something Allah has kept exclusively to His knowledge. The verse from al-Isra' is cited to show that only Allah knows its reality. Then the author addresses an objection: How could the Prophet not know the soul when he was given knowledge of all things? The answer is that the Prophet was informed of all that humans can know, not of Allah's total knowledge, and that verses like 'I do not know the unseen' refer to before revelation. The author then affirms that refraining from discussing the soul's essence is the chosen view. He then presents an additional detail: al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam held that there are two souls in each body – a soul of wakefulness and a soul of life – each with distinct functions. Finally, he mentions the pre-existence of souls at the covenant (Alastu bi-Rabbikum), with various orientations indicating different levels of love, as known to mystics like Sahl al-Tustari, and cites the hadith about souls being like troops that either harmonize or conflict based on recognition.

Key Terms
الروح
حقيقة
توقيف
كراهة
حرمة
المخاطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that discussing the soul is absolutely forbidden without exception.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars differ: the commentator considers it disliked (makruh), not forbidden, while al-Junayd considers it prohibited. The chosen view is to refrain, but the ruling is not absolute.

Checkpoint
Why did the commentator interpret the prohibition as makruh rather than haram?
A) The Prophet explicitly said it is makruh
B) There is no revealed instruction (tawqif) about the soul's reality
C) The soul is unimportant
D) It distracts from worship

Key Takeaway
While scholars differ on whether discussing the soul's essence is disliked or forbidden, the safest position is to refrain, as its true nature is known only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does your desire to understand the soul's essence reveal your attachment to worldly knowledge? Are you willing to submit to Allah's exclusive domain even when it leaves you with unanswered questions?
?
When you encounter a topic that is off-limits in your faith, like the soul's reality, do you feel a sense of loss or a sense of trust? What does your reaction say about the state of your tawakkul?
?
If the Prophet ﷺ did not know the soul's essence until Allah informed him, how much more should you be humbled? What practical changes can you make today to seek only the knowledge that benefits your faith?

Theories on the Soul: Multiplicity and Pre-Existence
Additional details on the soul: multiplicity and pre-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known view against multiple souls, al-'Izz's two-soul theory, and the pre-existence of souls as described in the Qur'an and hadith.

Core Idea
The soul's reality is hidden, but some scholars discuss its multiplicity and pre-existence based on revelation and experience.

This lesson continues from the previous prohibition, delving into what some scholars have said about the soul's multiplicity and pre-existence. The author notes the well-known view that there is not more than one soul per body, but then presents al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam's theory of two souls (wakefulness and life). Finally, the passage describes the pre-existent souls at the Covenant and the hadith about souls being like troops. This material provides a glimpse into speculative discussions that go beyond the basic prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the transition from the well-known view to the two-soul theory and then the pre-existence account.
The passage includes a quote from al-Yawaqit and a hadith.
2
Understand the well-known view
The author states that the well-known view is that there is only one soul per body. This is the default position.
3
Examine the two-soul theory
Al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam explicitly says there are two souls: one for wakefulness and one for life. Understand their functions and how they differ.
This is a minority view mentioned for completeness.
4
Explore pre-existence of souls
The passage describes souls on the Day of Covenant with various orientations. This indicates they existed before bodies. How does this relate to the hadith about souls being like troops?
5
Connect to spiritual recognition
The mention of Sahl al-Tustari recognizing his students from that time shows how some mystics claim knowledge of pre-existent connections.

A) The soul of wakefulness causes sleep, while the soul of life causes death
B) The soul of wakefulness is for consciousness, the soul of life for biological life
C) Both are identical but have different names
D) Only the soul of life is present at the Covenant

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-`Izz’s two‑soul theory separates the soul of wakefulness (consciousness) from the soul of life (biology). If your consciousness can leave during sleep while your life persists, what does that tell you about the true self? How should this detachment from worldly consciousness reshape your priorities today?

The Soul: Refraining from Speculation and the Two-Soul Theory
Sanusi states the ruling on not delving into the soul and presents al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's two-soul theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling on delving into the soul's reality and the two-soul theory of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam as a minority view.

Core Idea
Refrain from speculating on the soul; a minority theory posits two souls.

The soul is a mystery known only to Allah. While the Prophet was given knowledge of what is accessible to humans, the preferred view is to avoid speculative investigation. Yet some scholars, like al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, have proposed theories about the soul's nature, including the idea of two souls. This passage presents both the general ruling and the specific theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice that it begins with the limits of human knowledge about the soul and ends with the two-soul theory.
Pay attention to the phrase 'المختار' (the preferred view) and 'المشهور' (the well-known view).
2
Identify the two key points
The passage makes two main points: (1) the ruling to refrain from investigating the soul's essence, and (2) al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's explicit statement about two souls. Distinguish between the general principle and the specific opinion.
The well-known view is that there is only one soul per body.
3
Understand the two souls
Al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam describes two souls: the soul of wakefulness (rūḥ al-yaqaẓa) and the soul of life (rūḥ al-ḥayāt). Note how each is defined by its function in waking/sleeping and life/death.
These souls are interior; their location is known only to Allah.
4
Reflect on the tension
Consider how the passage holds together: the ruling to refrain from speculation is not contradicted by al-Izz's theory, because he is not speculating but transmitting what Allah revealed to him. The passage shows that the prohibition is on unaided reasoning, not on revealed knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرء حيث لم يعلم حقيقة نفسه التي بين جنبيه مع القطع بوجودها، ولم يخرج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدنيا حتى أطلعه الله تعالى على جميع ما أبهمه عنه من الروح وغيرها، مما يمكن علم البشر به لا على جميع معلوماته تعالى وإلا لزم مساواة الحادث للقديم، وما خالف ذلك نحو: ﴿وَلَا أَعْلَمُ الْغَيْبَ﴾ محمول على أنه كان قبل أن يُكشف له عن ذلك، وما ذكر من عدم الخوض في الروح هو المختار؛ ولذلك صدر الناظم به فنمسك عن بيان حقيقتها، وبيان مقرّها من الجسد والمشهور عدم تعدّدِ الروح في كل جسد، وصَرَّح العِزُّ بن عبد السلام بأن في كل جسد روحين: إحداهما: روحُ اليقظة التي أجرى الله العادة بأنها إذا كانت في الجسد كان الإنسان مستيقظاً، فإذا خرجت منه نام ورأت تلك الروح المنامات. والأخرى: روحُ الحياة التي أجرى الله العادة بأنها إذا كانت في الجسد كان حيا، وإذا فارقته مات وهاتان الروحان في باطن الإنسان لا يعرف مقرَّهما إلا مَنْ أطلعه الله على ذلك.
A person does not know the reality of his soul which is between his sides despite certainty of its existence, and the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, did not leave this world until God Almighty informed him of all that He had concealed from him regarding the soul and other matters, of what is possible for human knowledge—not of all of God's information, otherwise the temporal would equal the eternal. And what contradicts that, like {And I do not know the unseen} [Al-An'am 6:50], is interpreted to mean before it was revealed to him. And what is mentioned of refraining from delving into the soul is the preferred view; therefore the author began with it, so we refrain from explaining its reality and explaining its location in the body. The well-known view is that there is no multiplicity of souls in each body, but al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam explicitly stated that in each body there are two souls: one is the soul of wakefulness, by which God's custom runs that when it is in the body the person is awake, and when it leaves him he sleeps and that soul sees dreams. The other is the soul of life, by which God's custom runs that when it is in the body the person is alive, and when it leaves him he dies. These two souls are inside the person; no one knows their location except one whom God has informed of that.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a combination of ruling and exposition. It begins by establishing that a person does not know the essence of his own soul despite being certain of its existence, and that the Prophet was informed by God of what is accessible to human knowledge about it—but not all divine knowledge, as that would imply equality between the temporal and the eternal. Any scriptural evidence suggesting otherwise, such as the verse 'I do not know the unseen', is understood to refer to a time before such knowledge was revealed. The preferred position is to refrain from investigating the soul's reality, which is why the author of the poem (Ibn al-Muqri') began his discussion with this principle; accordingly, the commentator refrains from explaining its essence or its location in the body. The well-known view is that there is only one soul per body, but al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam explicitly states there are two: the soul of wakefulness (rūḥ al-yaqaẓa), which when present causes wakefulness and when it leaves causes sleep and dreams, and the soul of life (rūḥ al-ḥayāt), which when present gives life and when it leaves causes death. Both souls are internal and their location is known only to God. The commentary thus preserves the tension between the general prohibition on speculation and the specific transmitted opinion of a leading scholar.

Key Terms
الروح
روح اليقظة
روح الحياة
تعدّد الروح
العز بن عبد السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that investigating the soul is categorically forbidden, leaving no room for any discussion.
Correction: The passage shows that some scholars (like al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam) did discuss the soul, and the prohibition is on speculative reasoning without textual basis, not on revealed knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the well-known view regarding the number of souls in each body?
A) There are two souls: one of wakefulness and one of life.
B) There is only one soul.
C) There are three souls: animal, human, and spiritual.
D) The number of souls varies from person to person.

Key Takeaway
The soul's essence is beyond human knowledge, so the preferred view is to refrain from speculation; however, al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's two-soul theory is a transmitted minority opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been certain of your soul's existence your whole life, yet you do not know its reality. Does this not humble your confidence in your own self-knowledge? Will you accept the limit of your knowledge or continue to speculate about what Allah has veiled?
?
The Prophet, with all his rank, did not know the soul except what Allah chose to reveal. How then do you dare to fill your heart with opinions about matters Allah has not made known? What speculations about the unseen do you need to abandon today?
?
Al-Izz's theory of two souls is transmitted, but the preferred path is silence. Are you willing to prefer silence over curiosity when it comes to matters Allah has kept hidden? What would that cost you, and what might it gain you?

The Souls on the Day of Alast and the Hadith on Mutual Affinity
Description of souls' encounter on Day of Alast, the soldiers hadith, and the rule of textual sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional account of the souls' encounter during the primordial covenant and the basis for limiting theological speculation on such matters.

Core Idea
Pre-eternal affinities shape earthly bonds; silence where revelation is silent.

This lesson explores a fascinating report about the state of souls on the Day of Alast—some facing, some turning away—and the famous hadith that souls are like soldiers. It also clarifies why the tradition cautions against speculation when no explicit text exists. Together, these points illustrate how scriptural evidence and philosophical restraint guide Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three positions of the souls and the hadith.
2
Understand the primordial encounter
The passage describes souls facing each other, turning backs, or placing sides. This indicates degrees of pre-eternal love and aversion that prefigure earthly relationships.
Consider why this might be significant for human connections.
3
Reflect on the hadith
The hadith 'Souls are like conscripted soldiers' reinforces the idea that recognition or non-recognition from the Day of Alast determines affinity or conflict in this world.
This hadith is often quoted in Sufi and theological contexts.
4
Learn the epistemological principle
The passage ends by justifying why one should not delve into such matters without a clear text from the Lawgiver. This is a key principle in Islamic theology: avoid speculation where revelation is silent.
This connects to the broader methodology of the Salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان بعض الأرواح يومَ أَلَسْتُ بِرَبِّكُمْ مقبلاً على بعض بالوجه، وبعضها مُولّياً ظهره لبعض، وبعضها جاعلاً جنبه لبعض، فالإقبال بالوجه غاية في المودة وعكسه بالظهر، وبالجنب بين ذلك، كما في «اليواقيت»، قال: ويُكشف لكثير عن ذلك كسهل بن عبد الله، حتى إنهم يعرفون تلامذتهم إذ ذاك، وفي الحديث: «الأرواح جُنودٌ مُجنَّدَةٌ، فما تعارف منها ائتلف، وما تناكر منها اختلف» قوله: (إِذْ مَا وَرَدًا نَضٌ عَنِ الشَّارع أي: لأنه لم يرد دليل عن الله تعالى ببيانها، وكل ما هو كذلك فالأولى عدم الخوض فيه، وهذا التعليل للنهي عن الخوض في الروح على الطريقة المختارة.
And on the day of 'Am I not your Lord?', some souls were facing others with their faces, some turning their backs to others, and some placing their sides to others. So facing with the face is extreme in affection, the opposite with the back, and the side is between that, as in 'al-Yawāqīt'. He said: And much is unveiled about that to many, like Sahl ibn Abd Allah, so that they recognize their students from that time. And in the hadith: 'Souls are like conscripted soldiers; those who recognize each other bond, and those who do not recognize each other differ.' His statement: 'when no text has come from the Lawgiver' means: because no proof from God Almighty has come explaining it, and everything that is so, it is better not to delve into it. And this is the justification for the prohibition on delving into the soul according to the preferred method.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage first describes the state of souls in the primordial covenant (Alast) based on reports: some souls faced each other, some turned away, and some were side by side, indicating degrees of love and aversion that prefigure earthly relationships. It then cites Sahl ibn Abd Allah as someone who could perceive these distinctions and even recognize his students from that state. The hadith 'Souls are like conscripted soldiers' reinforces the idea that pre-existing familiarity or unfamiliarity dictates worldly affinity or conflict. The passage concludes with a comment on the poem's line 'when no text has come from the Lawgiver': meaning that in the absence of scriptural proof, one should not speculate about such matters—this is the rationale for the prohibition on investigating the soul, consistent with the preferred methodology. Thus the lesson connects the mystical vision of the covenant with the epistemological principle of textual sufficiency.

Misconception: One might think the hadith only refers to earthly friendships, but it is connected here to a pre-eternal covenant, suggesting a metaphysical basis for human relationships.
Correction: The hadith is interpreted in light of the Day of Alast, where souls were created and interacted, so that earthly affinity or aversion is rooted in that primordial encounter.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Souls have pre-eternal affections, and we should not speculate beyond revealed texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
On the Day of Alast, some souls faced you with affection and others turned their backs. When you consider your closest relationships today, do you see traces of that pre-eternal bond? Or have you neglected those whom Allah destined for you to love? What will you do today to strengthen those bonds?
?
The hadith says souls recognize each other and bond. Is there anyone in your life whom you feel a deep, inexplicable connection with? Have you thanked Allah for that blessing? And is there someone you dislike for no worldly reason? Could it be a pre-eternal unfamiliarity? How should that affect your treatment of them?
?
The lesson warns against delving into what Allah has not revealed. Are there questions about the unseen that you persistently chase, trying to know more than what the texts say? In your heart, are you content with what Allah has revealed, or do you secretly desire to uncover more? Repent from that and submit to the limits of revelation.

The Maliki View of the Soul and Its Locations
The author presents the Maliki view of the soul as a bodily form and cites various opinions on its location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maliki conception of the soul as a bodily form and the various traditional opinions about its location during life and after death, along with the methodological caution against excessive speculation.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body intertwined with the physical form, and its post-mortem abode varies by faith.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed presents the Maliki school's view on the nature of the soul, describing it as a form like the body. It then surveys a range of transmitted opinions about where the soul resides in life and after death. The author, however, reminds us that such speculation is not the preferred approach—what matters is accepting the transmitted reports without delving deeper.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the different opinions presented in a somewhat list-like manner.
2
Identify the different opinions
Identify the Maliki view of the soul as a body with limbs, then the subtle-body theory of Imam al-Haramayn, then the various locations during life and after death. Notice which opinions are attributed to which authorities.
The passage blends doctrinal exposition with a cautionary note.
3
Reflect on the methodological point
Consider why the author says 'the text by this chain suffices you.' What is the implied danger of delving too deeply into the soul's nature?
This connects to the broader theme that some matters are better left to transmitted texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات ٩٤ - لِمالِكِ هِي صُوْرَةٌ كَالجَسَدِ فَحَسْبُكَ النَّصُّ بِهَذَا السَّنَدِ قوله: لَكِنْ وُجِدَا لِمالِكِ هِي صُوْرَةٌ كَالْجَسَدِ) بسكون الياء لغة في (هي) بفتحها؛ أي: لكن وجد لأهل مذهب مالك ممَّن خاض في بيان حقيقة الروح هي: جسم ذو صورة كصورة الجسد في الشكل والهيئة، فإنَّ أصبغ نقل عن ابن القاسم عن عبد الرحيم بن خالد قال: (الروحُ ذو جسد ويدين ورجلين وعينين ورأس تُسَلُّ من الجسدِ سَلَّا)؛ وإنما نسبه المصنّف لمالك لاستنادهم إليه في ذلك، وما ذكر من الخوض في الروح هو غير المختار. قال النووي: وأصح ما قيل فيها على هذه الطريقة ما قاله إمام الحرمين: (إنها جسم لطيف شفافٌ مُشتبك بالجسم؛ كاشتباك الماء بالعود الأخضر، فتكون البدل). سارية في جميع [؟] وقيل: مقرها البطنُ، وقيل: القلب وقيل: بقرب القلب، والصواب ما قاله إمام الحرمين: (وهذا في حالة الحياة، وأما بعد الموت فأرواح السعداء بأفنية القبور على الصحيح). وقيل: عند آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام في سماء الدنيا لكن لا دائماً، فلا ينافي أنها تسرح حيث شاءت؛ وأما أرواح الكفار في سِجِّين في الأرض السابعة السفلى محبوسة. وقيل: أرواح السعداء بالجابية في الشام. وقيل: ببئر زمزم وأرواح الكفار ببئر بَرَهُوت في حضرموت التي هي مدينة في اليمن. وقوله: (فَحَسْبُكَ النَّصُّ بِهَذَا السَّنَدِ أي: إذا علمت النقل عن أهل مذهب مالك بالخوض في حقيقتها، فيكفيك في الخوض النصُّ عنهم حال كونه ملتبساً بهذا القول المسند إليهم من مُلابسة العام للخاص، فلا تخض بأكثر منه، فالمراد بالسند المسند
The Samiyyat: 94 - To Malik it is a form like the body, so the text by this chain suffices you. His statement: 'but it has been found for Malik that she is a form like the body' with sukūn on the yā' as a language variant of 'hiya' with fatḥa; meaning: but it has been found among the people of the Maliki school of those who delved into explaining the reality of the soul that it is a body possessing a form like the form of the body in shape and appearance. Indeed, Asbagh transmitted from Ibn al-Qasim from Abd al-Rahim ibn Khalid that he said: 'The soul is a body with hands, feet, eyes, and a head, and it is extracted from the body by extraction.' The author attributed it to Malik because they rely on him in that. And what is mentioned of delving into the soul is not the preferred view. Al-Nawawi said: The soundest view on this method is what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'It is a subtle, transparent body intertwined with the body, like the intertwining of water with a green branch, so it becomes the body.' It permeates the entire [body?]. And it was said: Its location is the belly, and it was said: the heart, and it was said: near the heart. The correct view is what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'This is in the state of life. As for after death, the souls of the blessed are in the gardens of the graves, according to the sound opinion.' And it was said: They are with Adam, peace be upon him, in the first heaven, but not permanently, so that does not contradict that they roam where they wish. As for the souls of disbelievers, they are held in Sijjin in the lowest seventh earth. And it was said: The souls of the blessed are in al-Jabiya in Syria. And it was said: At the well of Zamzam, and the souls of disbelievers are at the well of Barahut in Hadramawt, which is a city in Yemen. And his statement: 'so the text by this chain suffices you' means: if you know the transmission from the people of the Maliki school about delving into its reality, then the text from them, while being clothed with this statement attributed to them, suffices you for delving into it, like the clothing of the general from the specific, so do not delve more than that. What is meant by 'al-sanad' is 'al-musnad'.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents and evaluates various opinions about the soul's nature and location. It opens with a line from the poem: 'To Malik it is a form like the body, so the text by this chain suffices you.' The commentary explains that 'hiya' is a variant pronunciation, and the meaning is that among Malik's followers who discussed the soul's reality, it is considered a body with a form similar to the physical body, citing a report from Asbagh that the soul has a body, hands, feet, eyes, and a head and is extracted from the physical body. The author notes that this attribution to Malik is because those followers rely on him. However, the commentary immediately states that such delving is not the preferred view. It then cites al-Nawawi who mentions that the soundest view on this method is that of Imam al-Haramayn: the soul is a subtle, transparent body interwoven with the body like water with a fresh branch, so that it becomes the body itself. It permeates the entire body. Other opinions place its seat in the belly, heart, or near the heart, but the correct view is Imam al-Haramayn's regarding life. After death, the souls of the blessed are in the gardens of the graves (afniyat al-qubur), while other opinions place them with Adam in the first heaven (but not permanently), or in al-Jabiya in Sham, or at the well of Zamzam. The souls of disbelievers are confined in Sijjin in the lowest earth or at the well of Barahut in Hadramawt. Finally, the commentary explains the poem's closing line: if you have the transmitted report from Malik's school about the soul, that textual report suffices—do not delve further. Thus the passage surveys multiple transmitted opinions while reinforcing the methodological preference for textual reliance over speculative reasoning.

Correction: The passage presents this as one opinion but explicitly states that delving into the soul's reality is not the preferred approach. It then qualifies that the soundest view on this method is Imam al-Haramayn's subtle-body theory.

Key Takeaway
